حضرت امام ابو یوسف سے ایک روایت ہے کہ پہلے مسئلہ میں یعنی جب باندی خریدی اور مشتری کے قبضہ میں آ کر وہ کافی ہو گئی تو مشتری اس کو بغیر بیان کئے مرابحہ کے طور پر فروخت نہ کرے بلکہ اس تفصیل کو بیان کر دے کہ میں نے صحیح سالم باندی پر قبضہ کیا تھا لیکن میرے پاس آکر کافی ہو گئی ہے۔ جیسا کہ اگر مشتری اپنے فعل سے باندی کا کوئی جز فوت کر دے اور اس کو روک نے تو اس پر بیان کرنا ضروری ہے یہی قول امام شافعی اور امام زفر کا ہے۔ امام شافعی کے نزدیک بیان کرنا اس لیے ضروری ہے کہ ان کے نزدیک اوصاف
If a slave-girl is bought and she becomes one-eyed, or he has intercourse with her while she is a thayyib, on what basis can he sell her on murabahah? He said: And whoever buys a slave-girl and she becomes one-eyed, or he has intercourse with her while she is a thayyib, he sells her on murabahah and does not disclose, because he has not retained with him something that corresponds to the price. Indeed, attributes are subordinate; price is not in exchange for them. For this reason, if she dies before delivery, nothing of the price is waived. Similarly, the benefits of intercourse are not in exchange for the price. The issue is when intercourse did not decrease her. And from Abu Yusuf in the first case, that he should not sell from...

Translation: And if someone bought a slave-girl, then she became one-eyed, or he had intercourse with her while she was a thayyib, he can sell her on murabahah and does not disclose, because he does not have any such thing in his possession in exchange for which the price would be. For attributes are subordinate, price is not given in exchange for them. For this reason, if the eye died before delivery, nothing of the price is reduced. Similarly, the benefits of intercourse are not given in exchange for the price. And this issue is in the case that intercourse did not cause any defect. And in the first issue, it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that he should not sell without disclosure, as if something has been retained by the buyer's action. And that is the saying of al-Shafi'i.

Explanation: The scenario of the issue is that if someone bought a sound slave-girl, then she became one-eyed due to a heavenly calamity or by her own action or spontaneously, i.e., the vision of one eye was lost, or the slave-girl was a thayyib and the buyer had intercourse with her and no defect occurred due to intercourse, then if the buyer wants to sell this slave-girl on murabahah, he can sell, and it is not even obligatory for him to disclose that this slave-girl was sound when she came to me and became one-eyed.

The proof is that the buyer has not retained with him any thing in exchange of which the price would be. Because it has passed before that the soundness and defectiveness of the sold object are among the attributes, and attributes are subordinate to the essence; price is not given in exchange for them, rather price is given in exchange for the essence. And the essence of the thing, i.e., the slave-girl, exists. So due to the loss of the attribute of soundness, no part of the price will be reduced. And when no part of the price has been reduced, the buyer can do murabahah on the full price without disclosure. For the same reason, if before delivering the sold object to the buyer, an attribute of the sold object is lost while in the possession of the seller, the buyer does not have the right to reduce any part of the price; rather the buyer has the option to take or leave. Similarly, when an attribute of the sold object is lost while in the buyer's possession, no reduction will be made in the price; rather the buyer is permitted to do murabahah on the full price. Similarly, since the benefits of intercourse are not property, price is not given in exchange for them either. And due to the buyer's intercourse with the thayyib slave-girl, no part of the price of the slave-girl will be reduced, provided that intercourse did not cause any defect in the slave-girl. Rather the buyer is permitted to do murabahah on the full price. However, if the slave-girl was a virgin and the buyer had intercourse with her, then the buyer is not permitted to do murabahah on the full price without disclosure, because by removing the hymen, it is as if the buyer has retained a part of the slave-girl in his possession. And when the buyer retains a part of the sale, then surely a portion of the price will be reduced. And when a portion of the price has been reduced, how can murabahah on the full price be done without disclosure.

There is a narration from Imam Abu Yusuf that in the first issue, i.e., when the slave-girl is bought and she becomes one-eyed after coming into the buyer's possession, the buyer should not sell her on murabahah without disclosure; rather he should disclose the detail that 'I took possession of the slave-girl sound, but after coming to me she became one-eyed.' Just as if the buyer destroys a part of the slave-girl by his action and retains it, then disclosure is necessary. This is the saying of Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Zufar. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, it is necessary to disclose because according to him, attributes...
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage addresses whether a buyer who purchased a sound slave-girl can later sell her on murabahah without disclosing that she became defective while in his possession (e.g., lost an eye or was no longer a virgin due to intercourse). The ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa is that he may sell on murabahah without disclosure, provided the defect did not arise from a reduction in the essence. The proof: Defects are attributes (awṣāf) that are subordinate to the essence (dhāt); price is paid for the essence, not for attributes. For example, if the slave-girl dies before delivery, no part of the price is refunded. Similarly, the benefit of intercourse (manāfiʿ al-budʿ) is not compensable by price. Therefore, since no part of the price was retained by the buyer, he may sell at the full price without disclosure. However, this applies only when the intercourse did not cause any decrease in the slave-girl. In contrast, if the slave-girl was a virgin and the buyer had intercourse, he must disclose because he has effectively retained a part (the hymen) that carries value. In that case, full-price murabahah without disclosure is not allowed. There is also a narration from Abū Yūsuf that in the first scenario (one-eyed), the buyer should not sell without disclosure; he must disclose that the slave-girl was sound on receipt but became defective later. This is similar to the rule when the buyer's own action causes a loss. Imam al-Shāfiʿī and Imam Zufar hold the same view because they consider attributes as having compensatory value. The relied-upon (muftā bihī) position is that of Imam Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
جاریہ (jariyah): A female slave.
کانی (kaani): One-eyed (having lost sight in one eye).
ثیب (thayyib): A previously married woman (non-virgin).
مرابحہ (murabahah): A sale in which the seller discloses the cost and profit margin to the buyer.
بیان (bayan): Disclosure of defects or history of the item being sold.
اوصاف (awsaf): Attributes or qualities of a commodity, e.g., being sound or defective.
ذات (dhat): The essence or substance of an item, as opposed to its attributes.
منافع بضع (manafi' al-bud'): The benefits or pleasures derived from sexual intercourse with a slave-girl.
بکارت (bikarah): Virginity; the hymen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this lesson, when can a seller sell a defective slave-girl on murabahah without disclosing the defect?
A) Only if the defect is a part of the slave-girl.
B) Only if the defect is an attribute.
C) Only if the buyer caused the defect.
D) Only if the seller received no profit.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi murabahah, attribute defects do not require disclosure, but loss of a part does; the key is whether the seller has retained something of value.

intermediate

Murabahah Sale of Defective Slave Girl: When Disclosure is Not Required
Ruling on Murabahah sale of defective slave girl without disclosure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a defective commodity on Murabahah without disclosure and explain the principle that attributes are not compensable by price.

Core Idea
Attributes are subordinate to essence; a Murabahah seller need not disclose a defect he did not cause.

In Murabahah, honesty about cost is paramount, but what about defects that appear after purchase without the seller's fault? This passage from al-Hidayah explores the Hanafi view that certain defects need not be disclosed because price is for the essence, not attributes. We will examine the proofs and the ikhtilaf with other scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Read the passage to identify the exact scenario: a buyer purchases a sound slave girl, and after possession she becomes defective (e.g., blind in one eye) due to an act of God or without the buyer's action. The Hanafi ruling is that he may sell her on Murabahah without disclosing the defect.
The defect must be non-caused; if the buyer caused it, disclosure is required.
2
Locate the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The passage provides the proof: attributes (such as health, sight) are subordinate (tabi') to the essence (ayn) of the commodity. Price (thaman) is paid for the essence, not for attributes. Since the essence is still present, no part of the price is lost, so the seller has not retained anything compensable by price.
The same logic applies to benefits like intercourse: they are not wealth, so no price reduction.
3
Compare with the exception: buyer-caused defects
The passage notes an exception: if the buyer destroys virginity (a retained part), he must disclose because he has retained a part of the commodity. This confirms the principle that only when the buyer retains a compensable part must he disclose.
This contrasts with Shafi'i view where any defect, regardless of cause, requires disclosure.
4
Analyze the opposing views
Imam Abu Yusuf (in one narration), Imam al-Shafi'i, and Imam al-Zufar hold that even for non-caused defects, disclosure is necessary. Abu Yusuf analogizes to buyer-caused defects; al-Shafi'i argues attributes are compensable; al-Zufar says the second buyer may be misled about the profit margin.
The text does not provide a detailed Hanafi rebuttal but implicitly defends the view that no part is retained.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The text follows the Hanafi view (Imam Abu Hanifa) as the relied-upon position. In practice, Hanafi muftis rule that non-caused defects need not be disclosed in Murabahah.
This is an established position in the Hanafi school, supported by the principle 'al-awsaf tabi'ah li al-dhat' (attributes are subordinate to essence).

کتاب البيو
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ہشتم کے مقابلہ میں شمن ہوتا ہے عیب خواہ آسمانی آفت سے پیدا ہوا ہو یا بندہ کے فعل سے پیدا ہوا ہو ۔ اور امام زفر کے نزدیک اس لیے ضروری ہے کہ اگر مشتری ثانی کو یہ معلوم ہوا کہ مشترکی اول نے اس کو بغیر عیب کے خریدا ہے اور جوشن ذکر کیا ہے اس کے عوض خریدا ہے تو مشتہر کی اول کے لیے اس ثمن پر نفع لینا جائز نہیں جب تک کہ وہ عیب دار ہونے کے بعد متعین نہ کرے اس سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ امام زفر کے نزدیک مرابحہ بیچنے کے لیے اس تفصیل کا بیان کرنا ضروری ہے۔
Book of Sales. Explanation of when something is retained. 268. Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu commentary on Hidaya, volume 8. And it is the saying of al-Shafi'i. Translation: If someone bought a slave girl, then she became blind in one eye, or had intercourse with her while she was non-virgin, he may sell her on Murabahah and not disclose, because he does not have anything in return for which there is a price (thaman), and attributes are subordinate, not met with price. Similarly, if before delivery an eye was lost, nothing from the price is deducted. Similarly, benefits of sexual enjoyment are not compensated by price. This issue is when the master did not cause any damage. In the first issue, Abu Yusuf narrated that he should not sell without disclosing, as if something was retained by the buyer's action, and this is the saying of al-Shafi'i. Explanation: Scenario: If someone bought a sound slave girl, then she became blind from a calamity, or her own action, or spontaneously, or she was non-virgin and the buyer had intercourse without causing damage, then if he wants to sell on Murabahah he can, and it is not obligatory to disclose. Proof: The buyer has not retained anything in return for price, because soundness and defect are attributes, attributes are subordinate to essence, price is for the essence, and the essence is present, so loss of attribute does not reduce price. Similarly, if before delivery the seller loses an attribute, the buyer cannot deduct but has option. Similarly, benefits of intercourse are not wealth, so no price reduction. However, if she was virgin and he had intercourse, he must disclose because he retained a part. Abu Yusuf's narration: In the first issue, he should not sell without disclosing but should explain that he took possession sound but became blind. This is also the view of al-Shafi'i and Zufar. Al-Shafi'i: disclosure necessary because attributes are compensable. Al-Zufar: disclosure necessary because second buyer learns that first bought without defect and wants profit, so he must clarify.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from al-Hidayah regarding Murabahah (cost-plus) sale of a slave girl who became defective after purchase without the buyer's fault. The Hanafi position (based on Imam Abu Hanifa) is that the buyer may sell on Murabahah without disclosing the defect, because attributes (like health, sight) are not compensable by price; only the essence of the commodity commands price. The same applies if the buyer had intercourse with a non-virgin slave girl, as intercourse benefit is not wealth. However, if the buyer destroys virginity or causes a defect himself (or receives compensation for it), then disclosure is required. Imam Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and al-Zufar disagree, holding that even for non-caused defects, disclosure is necessary. The commentary follows the Hanafi position but records the ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
مرابحہ (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a known profit
ثمن (thaman): The price paid for a commodity
وصف (wasf): Attribute or quality of a thing (e.g., health, sight)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, if a buyer purchases a sound slave girl and she later becomes blind in one eye due to an act of God, may he sell her on Murabahah without disclosing the blindness?
A) Yes, because attributes are not compensable by price and he did not retain any part of the commodity
B) No, because blindness is a defect that must always be disclosed
C) Yes, but only if he also had intercourse with her
D) No, because he must disclose all changes to the second buyer

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi Murabahah, non-caused defects need not be disclosed because attributes are not compensated by price.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Murabahah Disclosure: Retention of Part by the Seller
Ruling on Murabahah sale when buyer causes defect or retains part

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Murabahah disclosure when the buyer causes a defect or retains a part, and apply the distinction between external and buyer-caused damage.

Core Idea
In Murabahah, disclosure is wajib only when the seller has retained a part of the commodity.

This passage from al-Hidayah completes the discussion on Murabahah sales involving defects. It establishes a key principle: if the buyer (now seller) has retained any part of the commodity, either by causing a defect or by receiving compensation for a third party's damage, he must disclose that when selling on Murabahah. The same applies if he removed the virginity of a slave girl. In contrast, damage from external causes like mice or fire does not require disclosure, because nothing was retained.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cases and their rulings
List the four scenarios: gouging eye (disclosure), intercourse with virgin (disclosure), mouse/fire damage (no disclosure), tearing from folding (disclosure).
2
Locate the underlying principle
The common thread is 'retaining a part of the commodity' – either the defective part itself (eye, hymen) or its compensation (arsh).
3
Understand the rational proof
The destroyed attribute becomes 'maqsud' (intended), so it corresponds to a portion of the price. Retaining that portion (or its value) triggers disclosure.
4
Apply the distinction
Distinguish between buyer-caused damage (disclosure required) and external damage (no disclosure), because only in the former does the seller retain something.
5
Note the mufta bihi
Disclosure is wajib in all cases where the seller retains a part, whether by his own act or by receiving arsh from a third party.

کپڑا خریدا چوہے نے اسے کاٹ دیا یا آگ نے جلا دیا کتنے میں مرابحتا بیچ سکتا ہے
ولو اشترى ثوبا فاصابه فرض فار او حرق ناريبيعه مرابحة من غير بيان ولو تكسر بنشره وطيه لا يبيعه حتى يبين والمعنى ما بيناه
ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے کپڑا خریدا پھر اس کو چوہے نے کا نا یا آگ نے جلایا تو بغیر بیان کئے اس کو مرابحہ سے فروخت کر سکتا ہے
If he himself or a stranger gouged her eye and the buyer received compensation, then he cannot sell her on Murabahah until he discloses, because it became an intentional destruction, so a portion of price corresponds to it. Similarly, if he had intercourse with her while she was virgin, because the hymen is a part of the essence corresponding to price, and he has retained it. Translation: If the buyer himself gouged the eye or a stranger gouged it and the buyer received compensation, he cannot sell on Murabahah until he discloses, because the attribute became compensable. Similarly, if he had intercourse with a virgin, because the hymen is part of the essence. Explanation: If buyer gouged eye himself or stranger did and buyer took compensation, disclosure is obligatory because it is like retaining a part. Similarly, if he had intercourse with a virgin, disclosure required because he retained a part. Cloth: If someone bought a cloth and a mouse cut it or fire burnt it, he may sell on Murabahah without disclosure. If it tore from folding/pressing, he cannot sell without disclosure. The meaning is as we explained.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage completes the discussion on Murabahah sales of slave girls and extends it to cloth. It states the general principle: if the buyer causes a defect (e.g., gouging an eye) or receives compensation for a defect caused by another, he must disclose it when selling on Murabahah, because he has effectively retained a part of the commodity (the eye or its value). Similarly, if he has intercourse with a virgin slave girl, destroying her hymen, he must disclose that he removed her virginity, because the hymen is a part of the essence that commands a price. The cloth analogy reiterates: damage by external causes (mouse, fire) does not require disclosure, but damage caused by the buyer's action (folding/pressing) does require disclosure.

Key Terms
مقصود (maqsud): An attribute that becomes intended and compensable due to destruction
عذرة (udhra): Virginity, specifically the hymen as part of the essence of a slave girl
ارش (arsh): Compensation paid for a defect; damages for injury

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if a buyer gouges out the eye of a slave girl he purchased, and then wants to sell her on Murabahah, what must he do?
A) He may sell without disclosure, as attributes are not compensable
B) He must disclose the gouging because he retained a part of the commodity
C) He must sell at a reduced price but need not disclose
D) He cannot sell her at all on Murabahah

Key Takeaway
Disclosure in Murabahah is wajib only when the seller has retained a part of the commodity, whether physically or through compensation; external damage without retention does not require disclosure.

Disclosure of Defects in Murabahah: The Case of Damage by Buyer
Ruling with proof on disclosure of buyer-caused damage in Murabahah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi ruling on when a Murabahah seller must disclose damage caused to the item, and EXPLAIN the legal reasoning (istihsan) based on intended attributes.

Core Idea
In Murabahah, buyer must disclose any damage to an intended attribute because the price includes that attribute.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Al-Hidayah on Murabahah sales. When a buyer damages the merchandise (e.g., putting out an eye or deflowering a virgin slave-girl) and then sells it on Murabahah, he must disclose that damage. The reason is that the damaged attribute is 'wasf maqsud' – an intended quality that corresponds to a portion of the price. Without disclosure, the sale would involve deception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is: buyer damages the item (blinding or deflowering) then sells on Murabahah. The ruling: disclosure of that damage is wajib before the sale.
Failure to disclose gives the buyer the option to rescind.
2
Understand the key concept 'wasf maqsud'
An intended attribute (e.g., vision, virginity) that is normally valued in the market and contributes to the price. It is part of the thaman.
Only attributes that are 'maqsud' are treated this way, not trivial defects.
3
Trace the dalil (istihsan)
The text states: 'because it becomes an intended purpose, a portion of the price corresponds to it.' This is istihsan – preserving fairness by preventing hidden commission.
No explicit Quran/Sunnah text; rationale based on the nature of Murabahah as a trust sale.
4
Apply the ruling to analogous cases
Same rule applies if a third party causes the damage and buyer takes compensation, because retaining compensation is like retaining the part. Also applies to deflowering a virgin.
Key is whether buyer has effectively retained the value of the attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاما اذا فقاعينها بنفسه او فقاها اجنبى فاخذ ارشها لم يبعها مرابحة حتى يبين لانه صار مقصود ابا لا تلاف فيقابلها شيء من الثمن وكذا اذا وطيها وهى بكر لان العذرة جزء من العين يقابلها الثمن وقد حيسها. ترجمہ اگر مشتری نے خود اس کی آنکھ پھوڑ دی یا کسی اجنبی نے آنکھ پھوڑی اور مشتری نے اس سے جرمانہ وصول کر لیا تو وہ باندی کو مرابحہ سے فروخت نہیں کر سکتا یہاں تک کہ بیان کر دے کیونکہ تلف کرنے سے وصف مذکور مقصود ہو گیا تو اس کے مقابلہ میں شمن سے حصہ ہوگا اور اسی طرح اگر باندی سے وطی کی حالانکہ وہ با کر تھی اس لیے کہ پردہ بکارت باندی کی ذات کا ایک جز ہے جس کے مقابلہ میں شمن ہے۔ حالانکہ اس جز کو مشتری نے روک لیا ہے۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مشتری نے باندی کی آنکھ خود پھوڑی یا کسی اجنبی نے پھوڑی خواہ مشتری کے حکم سے یا بغیر حکم کے۔ اور مشترکی نے اس سے اس کا جرمانہ بھی وصول کر لیا تو اس باندی کو مرابحہ بیچنے کے لیے آنکھ پھوڑنے کا ذکر کرنا واجب ہے دلیل یہ ہے کہ آنکھ تلف کرنے کی وجہ سے بینائی کا وصف مقصود ہو گیا اور اوصاف مقصورہ کے مقابلہ میں شمن ہوتا ہے پس مشتری نے باندی کی آنکھ پھوڑ کر گویا باندی کے ایک جز کو اپنے پاس روک لیا ہے اور اجنبی کے پھوڑنے کی صورت میں چونکہ مشتری نے جرمانہ وصول کر لیا ہے اور بدل کا اپنے پاس روکنا مقصود علیہ کے جز کو روکنا ہے اور جس صورت میں مشترکی بیچ کا کوئی جزا اپنے پاس روک لے تو اس صورت میں بغیر بیان گئے مرابحہ جائز نہیں ہوتا اس لیے اس صورت میں آنکھ پھوڑنے کا ذکر کرنا ضروری ہے اسی طرح اگر مشترکی نے ولی کی درانحالیکہ باندی باکرہ ہے تو بھی بغیر بیان کئے مشتری کو مرابحہ کرنے کی اجازت نہیں ہے کیونکہ بکارت کا پردہ باندی کی ذات کا ایک جز ہے جس کے مقابلہ میں شمن ہوتا ہے حالانکہ اس جز کومشتری نے اپنے پاس روک لیا ہے اس لیے مرابحہ کرتے وقت یہ ضرور بیان کر دے کہ اس قدرشن کے عوض با کرہ تھی اس کا پردہ بکارت میں نے زائل کر دیا ہے
As for if he puts out its eye himself or a stranger puts it out and he takes the compensation, he may not sell it on Murabahah until he explains, because due to the destruction the intended attribute becomes a purpose, so a portion of the price corresponds to it. Similarly, if he has intercourse with it while it was a virgin, because the hymen is a part of the substance of the slave-girl, the price corresponds to it, and he has detained it. Commentary: If the buyer himself put out the eye, or a stranger put it out with or without the buyer's permission, and the buyer took compensation, then it is obligatory to mention the eye injury when selling on Murabahah. The evidence is that destroying the eye made the attribute of vision intended, and the price corresponds to intended attributes. Thus, by destroying the eye, the buyer has effectively retained a part of the slave-girl. In the case of a stranger, since the buyer took compensation, retaining the compensation is like retaining the part. Whenever the buyer retains any part of the sold item, selling on Murabahah without disclosure is impermissible. Similarly, if the buyer deflowered a virgin slave-girl, he must disclose that he removed the hymen because the hymen is a part of the substance corresponding to the price.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning Murabahah sale after the buyer damages the item. The ruling: If the buyer puts out the eye of a slave-girl (or a third party does so and the buyer takes compensation), he cannot sell it on Murabahah (cost-plus-profit) without disclosing that fact. The same applies if he deflowers a virgin slave-girl. The reason ('illa) is that by destroying that part, the attribute (vision or virginity) becomes an intended attribute (wasf maqsud), and in Murabahah, the price includes compensation for such attributes. Since the buyer has effectively retained the value of that part, selling without disclosure constitutes a hidden defect (khayanah). The Hanafi position requires full transparency; failure to disclose gives the buyer the option to rescind. The commentary expands by clarifying that even if a stranger caused the damage and the buyer took compensation, the same rule applies because retaining compensation is like retaining the part.

Key Terms
مرابحہ (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost price and adds a profit.
وصف مقصود (wasf maqsud): An attribute that is intended and has a value in the market, such as vision or virginity, which corresponds to a part of the price.
شمن (thaman): Price; the consideration in a sale contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, why must the buyer disclose that he put out the slave-girl's eye before selling on Murabahah?
A) Because the eye is a separate part of the slave-girl that was not included in the original sale.
B) Because the attribute of vision is an intended attribute that corresponds to a portion of the price, and the buyer has destroyed it.
C) Because the buyer is required to disclose all defects, even if they were present before purchase.
D) Because Murabahah is invalid if the seller has used the item at all.

Key Takeaway
In Murabahah, any damage that affects an intended attribute and is caused by or compensated to the buyer must be disclosed before resale.

Disclosure of Damage in Murabahah: Buyer's Action vs External Cause
Differentiating between damage by external cause (no disclosure) and damage by buyer's action (disclosure required) in Murabahah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between damage requiring disclosure and damage not requiring disclosure in Murabahah sales and explain the Hanafi legal reasoning based on intended attributes (wasf maqsud).

Core Idea
Only damage caused by the buyer's action creates an intended attribute that must be disclosed in Murabahah; external damage does not.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a crucial distinction in Murabahah transactions. When a cloth is damaged after purchase, the seller must know whether the damage was caused by an external event (like a mouse or fire) or by the buyer's own handling (like folding and unfolding). The Hanafi school applies the principle of intended attributes to determine disclosure requirements. Let us examine the ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note that the text contrasts two cases: damage by mouse/fire (external) vs tearing from folding/unfolding (buyer's action). The ruling for each is opposite: no disclosure vs disclosure required.
2
Locate the underlying principle
The text states 'al-ma'na ma bayyannahu' – the reason is what we have explained earlier. This refers to the earlier discussion about attributes not corresponding to price unless they become intended (maqsud).
Refer to the slave-girl analogy if needed.
3
Apply the principle to each scenario
In the external damage case, the attribute of soundness is not 'intended' because the buyer did not cause the loss; it remains a general attribute. In the folding tear case, the buyer's action destroyed the soundness, making it an intended attribute that has market value.
Intended attributes are those that affect the price when lost.
4
Derive the ruling
Since intended attributes correspond to a portion of the price, failing to disclose the tear is a deception (ghabn). Hence, disclosure is wajib. External damage does not create such an intended attribute, so no disclosure is needed.
This is based on istihsan, not qiyas.
5
Note cross-madhhab perspective
Some schools might require disclosure for any change in condition. The Hanafi distinction is based on causation.
The Hanafi view is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى ثوبا فاصابه فرض فار او حرق ناريبيعه مرابحة من غير بيان ولو تكسر بنشره وطيه لا يبيعه حتى يبين والمعنى ما بيناه. ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے کپڑا خریدا پھر اس کو چوہے نے کا نا یا آگ نے جلایا تو بغیر بیان کئے اس کو مرابحہ سے فروخت کر سکتا ہے اور اگر اس کے کھولنے لپیٹنے میں وہ پھٹ گیا تو اس کو (مرابحہ ) فروخت نہیں کر سکتا یہاں تک کہ بیان کر دے اور اس کی وجہ وہی ہے جو ہم بیان کر چکے۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے کپڑا خریدا اور پھر اس کو چوہے نے کاٹ دیا یا آگ نے جلا دیا تو مشتری اس کپڑے کو بغیر بیان کئے بیع مرابحہ کے طور پر فروخت کر سکتا ہے یعنی یہ بیان کرنا واجب نہیں ہے کہ یہ کپڑ ا خریدتے وقت صحیح سالم تھا بعد میں چوہے نے کاٹا ہے یا آگ نے جلایا ہے اور اگر کپڑا کھولتے اور لیٹتے وقت پھٹ گیا تو بیع مرابحہ کرتے وقت اس کا بیان کرنا ضروری ہے۔ دليل ... سابق میں گذر چکی کہ اوصاف بیع کے مقابلہ میں ثمن نہیں ہوتا لیکن اگر اوصاف مقصود ہو جائیں تو ان کے مقابلہ میں شمن ہوتا ہے۔ پس کھولنے اور پیٹنے سے جب کپڑا پھٹ گیا تو چونکہ اس کپڑے کے پھٹنے میں مشتری کے فعل کو دخل ہے اس لیے تلف کرنے کی وجہ سے کپڑے کا وصف سلامت مقصود ہو گیا اور جب وصف مقصود ہو گیا تو اس کے مقابلہ میں شمن ضرور ہو گا اور جب وصف کے مقابلہ میں شمن ہے تو بغیر بیان کئے مرابحہ بیچنا جائز نہ ہوگا۔
And if he buys a cloth and a mouse afflicts it or fire burns it, he can sell it on Murabahah without disclosure. But if it tears due to its unfolding and folding, he may not sell it until he discloses. The meaning is what we have explained. Commentary: If the cloth is damaged by mouse or fire, the buyer can sell on Murabahah without stating that it was damaged after purchase. But if it tears during unfolding and folding, disclosure is required. The evidence is that attributes (in themselves) do not correspond to price, but if they become intended, they do correspond. Since the tearing happened due to the buyer's action (unfolding/folding), the attribute of soundness becomes intended, so it corresponds to a portion of the price, and disclosure is necessary.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof on when a buyer must disclose damage to cloth in a Murabahah sale. If the cloth is damaged by an external cause (mouse or fire) with no involvement of the buyer, no disclosure is required because such attributes (original soundness) are not considered 'intended attributes' that affect the price, as they are not created by the buyer's action. However, if the cloth tears due to the buyer's own act of unfolding and folding, the attribute of soundness becomes an intended attribute (wasf maqsud) because the buyer's action destroyed it. Therefore, the buyer must disclose that the cloth was sound at purchase and he tore it himself. The underlying principle is that only attributes that are 'intended' (i.e., have market value and are affected by the seller's conduct) need disclosure. The proof (dalil) is based on istihsan: since the buyer's action made the attribute intended, it corresponds to a portion of the price, so failure to disclose constitutes deception.

Key Terms
وصف سلامت (wasf salamah): The attribute of being sound/undamaged; when it becomes intended due to the buyer's act, it corresponds to price.
وصف مقصود (wasf maqsud): An intended attribute that has market value; its loss due to buyer's action requires disclosure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is disclosure NOT required when a cloth is damaged by a mouse after purchase?
A) Because a mouse is a third party and the buyer did not cause the damage.
B) Because the attribute of soundness is not considered an intended attribute when the damage is external.
C) Because the buyer can claim compensation from the mouse owner.
D) Because the cloth is still fit for use.

Key Takeaway
In Murabahah, disclosure is only required for damages caused by the seller's own action because such damages create intended attributes that correspond to the price.

Buyer's Option for Non-Disclosure of Credit Term in Murabahah
Ruling on buyer's option when seller fails to disclose credit purchase in Murabahah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on buyer's option (khiyar) when the seller fails to disclose a credit purchase in a Murabahah sale and explain the legal analogy (qiyas) to a defect.

Core Idea
The credit term has value; hiding it is deception, giving the buyer the same option as a hidden defect.

In Murabahah sales, the seller must disclose the original purchase price and any terms that affect it. When the seller purchased on credit, that credit period (ajal) increases the cost. If the seller sells on Murabahah without revealing the credit, the buyer discovers a hidden price component. This lesson examines the Hanafi position that the buyer may rescind or affirm the sale, based on an analogy to defective goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the passage and determine what right the buyer has when the credit term is not disclosed. Note that the sale itself is valid, but the buyer gets an option.
The ruling: the buyer may return the item or keep it at the agreed price.
2
Understand the analogy (qiyas)
The text compares the credit term (ajal) to a commodity (mabi'). How does this analogy work? Think: ajal increases the price just as a separate item would, so nondisclosure is like selling two items for the price of one.
Analogy: it is as if the seller bought two things (slave and credit term) and sold one (slave) for the price of both.
3
Recognize the deception (khiyanah)
Murabahah is a trust-based sale. The seller commits khiyanah by hiding material facts. Identify what makes the credit term a material fact.
The price increase due to credit is significant; hiding it undermines trust.
4
Connect to khiyar al-ayb (option for defect)
The passage states the buyer is given the option 'as in the case of a defect'. Explain why nondisclosure is analogous to a hidden defect.
Both involve a hidden characteristic that affects the value and the buyer's consent.
5
Note the madhhab agreement
The commentary mentions that Imam Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad also hold this view. Consider the significance: this is not an area of major dispute.
This strengthens the Hanafi position through broad agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن اشترى غلا ما بالف درهم نسيئة فباعه بربح مائة ولم يبين فعلم المشترى فان شاء رده وان شاء قبل لان للاجل شبها بالمبيع الايرى انه يزاد فى الثمن لاجل الاجل والشبهة فى هذا ملحقه بالحقيقة فصار كانه اشترى شيأين وباع احدهما مرابحة بثمنهما والاقدام على المرابحة يوجب السلامة عن مثل هذه الخيانة فاذا ظهرت يخير كما في العيب. ترجمہ اگر کسی نے غلام ایک ہزار درہم کے عوض ادھار خریدا پھر اس کو ایک سوروپی نفع پر فروخت کیا اور ادھار خریدنے کا حال بیان نہیں کیا پھر مشتری کو یہ بات معلوم ہوئی پس اگر چاہے تو واپس کر دے اور اگر چاہے تو قبول کر لے۔ کیونکہ میعاد، بیج کے مشابہ ہے کیا نہیں دیکھتے ہو کہ میعاد کی وجہ سے ثمن میں اضافہ کیا جاتا ہے اور شبہ اس باب میں حقیقت کے ساتھ لاحق ہے پس ایسا ہو گیا گویا اس نے دو چیزیں خریدیں اور ان دونوں میں سے ایک کو ان دونوں کے شمن پر مرابحہ سے فروخت کیا حالانکہ مرابحہ پر اقدام کرنا اس جیسی خیانت سے سلامتی کو واجب کرتا ہے پس جب خیانت ظاہر ہوئی تو مشتری کو اختیار دیا جائے گا جیسا کہ عیب میں ہے۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی نے ایک غلام ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض ادھار خریدا پھر اس نے ایک سورو پسیہ کا نفع لے کر گیارہ سو روپیہ کے عوض مرابحہ کے طور پر نقد فروخت کر دیا اور یہ بیان نہیں کیا کہ میں نے ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض ادھار خریدا تھا پھر مشتری ثانی کو معلوم ہوا کہ مشتری اول یعنی میرے بائع نے ادھار خریدا ہے تو مشتری ثانی کو اختیار ہوگا کہ وہ بیع مرابحہ کو رد کر دے یا اس کو گیارہ سو یہ نقد کے عوض قبول کر لے۔ اس کے قائل امام شافعی اور امام احمد ہیں۔
He said: And if someone buys a slave for one thousand dirhams on credit, then sells it for a profit of one hundred without disclosing (the credit), and the buyer finds out, then he may return it if he wishes or accept it if he wishes, because the term (delay) resembles the commodity. Do you not see that the price is increased due to the term? And the resemblance in this is attached to reality, so it becomes as if he bought two items and sold one of them on Murabahah for the price of both. And proceeding upon Murabahah requires freedom from such deception. So when deception appears, he is given the option, just as in the case of a defect. Commentary: A man buys a slave for one thousand rupees on credit, then sells it for eleven hundred rupees cash (profit of one hundred) on Murabahah without disclosing that he bought it on credit. The second buyer learns of the credit term. The second buyer has the option to rescind the Murabahah sale or accept it for eleven hundred cash. This is the view of Imam Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning Murabahah when the seller fails to disclose that he purchased the item on credit. The Hanafi position (from the text of Al-Hidayah) is that the buyer has the option (khiyar) to rescind or affirm the sale because the credit term (ajal) is considered like a separate commodity (shabah bil-mabi'). Since the price is increased due to the credit term, nondisclosure constitutes deception. The analogy: it is as if the seller bought two things (the slave and the credit term) and sold one of them (the slave) for the combined price. The disclosure of the credit term is required to avoid khyanah (betrayal). The commentator states that this ruling is also held by Imam Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad, suggesting it is an area of agreement? However, the Hanafi position as given is that the buyer gets the option. The proof is rational (dalil aqli): the credit term affects the price, so hiding it is like hiding a defect. The passage is essentially presenting the Hanafi view that failure to disclose the credit term gives the buyer the right of rescission.

Key Terms
اجل (ajal): The fixed term or credit period in a deferred payment sale.
خیانت (khiyanah): Betrayal or deception; in Murabahah, failing to disclose material facts (like credit term) is considered khiyanah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the buyer have the option to rescind when the seller fails to disclose that he bought the item on credit?
A) Because credit sales are invalid.
B) Because the credit term is considered similar to a commodity and affects the price, so hiding it is deceptive.
C) Because the buyer must always be told the exact profit margin.
D) Because the seller acted without permission.

Key Takeaway
In Murabahah, the credit term has value; its nondisclosure is deceptive and gives the buyer the option to rescind the sale.

Disclosure of Defects in Murabaha: Intended vs External Attributes
Explains when tearing requires disclosure in Murabaha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between defects from external causes and those from the buyer's own action, and state the Hanafi rule that only intended attributes require disclosure in Murabaha sales.

Core Idea
In Murabaha, defects caused by the buyer's own action must be disclosed; external ones need not, because only intended attributes affect price.

This passage from the Hidayah clarifies a critical nuance in Murabaha sales: when a defect arises after purchase, must the seller disclose it? The answer depends on whether the defect resulted from the buyer's own action or an external cause. Understanding this distinction prevents trust-based sales from falling into dishonesty (khayanat).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling a cloth as Murabaha after it tore while the buyer was opening/folding it. The ruling is that such sale is not permissible (ja'iz nah hoga) unless the defect is disclosed. The ruling level is makruh tahrimi.
The text contrasts this with external defects (mouse bite, fire) where disclosure is not obligatory.
2
Locate the distinguishing criterion
The key criterion is whether the buyer's own action caused the defect. If the buyer's action contributed (e.g., tearing while handling), the attribute of soundness becomes 'intended' (maqsud). If the cause is external, the attribute was not intended at the time of purchase.
Intention (qasd) is central: only intended attributes are compensated in the price.
3
Trace the reasoning (ta'lil)
The default rule: attributes (wasf) are not against the price (thaman) – meaning the price is not considered to cover them unless they are intended. When the buyer's action destroys the attribute, it becomes intended, so part of the price is allocated to it. Concealing such a defect cheats the new buyer.
This is a qiyas: the attribute of soundness is akin to part of the goods; its loss due to buyer's action implicates the price.
4
Apply to the given case
Since tearing occurred while the buyer opened and folded the cloth, the buyer's action caused the loss of soundness. Hence soundness became an intended attribute, and the seller must disclose it in a Murabaha sale. Without disclosure, the sale is prohibited.
If the tear had occurred from a mouse bite, the buyer had no control, so no disclosure needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اور اگر اس کے کھولنے لپیٹنے میں وہ پھٹ گیا تو اس کو (مرابحہ) فروخت نہیں کر سکتا یہاں تک کہ بیان کر دے اور اس کی وجہ وہی ہے جو ہم بیان کر چکے۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے کپڑا خریدا اور پھر اس کو چوہے نے کاٹ دیا یا آگ نے جلا دیا تو مشتری اس کپڑے کو بغیر بیان کئے بیع مرابحہ کے طور پر فروخت کر سکتا ہے یعنی یہ بیان کرنا واجب نہیں ہے کہ یہ کپڑا خریدتے وقت صحیح سالم تھا بعد میں چوہے نے کاٹا ہے یا آگ نے جلایا ہے اور اگر کپڑا کھولتے اور لیٹتے وقت پھٹ گیا تو بیع مرابحہ کرتے وقت اس کا بیان کرنا ضروری ہے۔ دلیل سابق میں گذر چکی کہ اوصاف بیع کے مقابلہ میں ثمن نہیں ہوتا لیکن اگر اوصاف مقصود ہو جائیں تو ان کے مقابلہ میں ثمن ہوتا ہے۔ پس کھولنے اور پیٹنے سے جب کپڑا پھٹ گیا تو چونکہ اس کپڑے کے پھٹنے میں مشتری کے فعل کو دخل ہے اس لیے تلف کرنے کی وجہ سے کپڑے کا وصف سلامت مقصود ہو گیا اور جب وصف مقصود ہو گیا تو اس کے مقابلہ میں ثمن ضرور ہو گا اور جب وصف کے مقابلہ میں ثمن ہے تو بغیر بیان کئے مرابحہ بیچنا جائز نہ ہوگا۔
And if it tore while opening and folding, he cannot sell it as Murabaha unless he discloses, and the reason is the same as we have explained. Explanation: If someone bought a cloth and then a mouse bit it or fire burned it, the buyer can sell that cloth as Murabaha without disclosure, meaning it is not obligatory to state that the cloth was sound at purchase but later was bitten by mouse or burnt by fire. And if the cloth tore while opening and folding, then at the time of Murabaha sale, its disclosure is necessary. The proof has passed earlier that attributes are not against the price in the sale, but if attributes become intended, then they are against the price. So when the cloth tore from opening and folding, since the buyer's action contributed to the tearing, due to this destruction, the attribute of soundness became an intended attribute. And when the attribute became intended, then there is definitely a price against it. And when price is against the attribute, then without disclosure, selling as Murabaha is not permissible.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining when a defect in an item must be disclosed in a Murabaha sale. The key distinction is whether the defect occurred due to an external cause (like a rat bite or fire) or due to the buyer's own action (like tearing while opening/folding). If the defect is from an external cause, the attribute of soundness was not intended at the time of purchase, so it does not affect the price and disclosure is not required. However, if the defect is from the buyer's action, then the attribute of soundness becomes an intended attribute, meaning the buyer implicitly paid for that attribute. Therefore, selling without disclosure would be cheating (khayanat), and it is impermissible. The ruling follows the Hanafi principle that only intended attributes are compensated in the price.

Key Terms
مرابحہ (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the original cost and adds a known profit.
ثمن (thaman): Price or consideration in a sale.
وصف (wasf): Attribute or quality of the item sold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person buys a cloth and later a mouse bites it. He sells it as Murabaha without mentioning the bite. According to Hanafi fiqh, this sale is:
A) Valid and permissible without disclosure.
B) Invalid unless he discloses the bite.
C) Valid only if he reduces the profit.
D) Makruh tahrimi and the buyer has no option.

Key Takeaway
In Murabaha sales, only defects caused by the seller's own action after purchase require disclosure; external defects do not, because the price does not cover unintended attributes.

Option to Rescind Murabaha When Credit is Undisclosed
Ruling with proof via qiyas on the resemblance of credit to a commodity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Murabaha when credit is undisclosed and explain the qiyas reasoning that gives the buyer the option.

Core Idea
In Murabaha, credit is like property; concealing it gives the buyer the option to rescind.

Murabaha is a trust-based sale where the seller must disclose all cost-affecting factors. One such factor is whether the seller purchased the item on credit, as credit increases the effective cost. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling when the seller fails to disclose that the purchase was on credit, and the buyer discovers this after the sale. The passage uses a clear qiyas reasoning to establish the buyer's option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Read the scenario: a slave bought on credit for 1000, sold as Murabaha for 1100 without disclosure. The buyer learns. State the ruling: the buyer has the option to rescind or accept.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is qiyas: credit (ajal) resembles the thing sold because it affects the price. The text says 'because the term has resemblance to the thing sold. Do you not see that the price is increased due to the term?'
3
Analyze the analogy (qiyas)
The original case (asl) is selling two items together: the slave and the credit. The new case (far') is selling the slave alone in Murabaha without accounting for credit. The illa (effective cause) is that credit is a property-like element that increases price. Therefore, concealing it is like concealing part of the subject matter.
The text says: 'So it becomes as if he bought two things and sold one of them as Murabaha at the price of both.'
4
Relate to khiyar al-ayb
The option is analogous to the option due to defect (khiyar al-ayb). Just as a hidden defect gives the buyer a choice, so does hidden credit because it is a form of betrayal (khiyanah).
5
Note the cross-madhhab agreement
The passage states that Imams Shafi'i and Ahmad also hold this view.
This strengthens the ruling's authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غلام ادھار ایک ہزار سے خرید اسو کے نفع سے فروخت کر دیا اور بیان نہیں کیا تو مشتری رد کر سکتا ہے یا نہیں قال ومن اشترى غلا ما بالف درهم نسيئة فباعه بربح مائة ولم يبين فعلم المشترى فان شاء رده وان شاء قبل لان للاجل شبها بالمبيع الايرى انه يزاد فى الثمن لاجل الاجل والشبهة فى هذا ملحقه بالحقيقة فصار كانه اشترى شيأين وباع احدهما مرابحة بثمنهما والاقدام على المرابحة يوجب السلامة عن مثل هذه الخيانة فاذا ظهرت يخير كما في العيب ترجمہ اگر کسی نے غلام ایک ہزار درہم کے عوض ادھار خریدا پھر اس کو ایک سوروپی نفع پر فروخت کیا اور ادھار خریدنے کا حال بیان نہیں کیا پھر مشتری کو یہ بات معلوم ہوئی پس اگر چاہے تو واپس کر دے اور اگر چاہے تو قبول کر لے۔ کیونکہ میعاد، بیع کے مشابہ ہے کیا نہیں دیکھتے ہو کہ میعاد کی وجہ سے ثمن میں اضافہ کیا جاتا ہے اور شبہ اس باب میں حقیقت کے ساتھ لاحق ہے پس ایسا ہو گیا گویا اس نے دو چیزیں خریدیں اور ان دونوں میں سے ایک کو ان دونوں کے شمن پر مرابحہ سے فروخت کیا حالانکہ مرابحہ پر اقدام کرنا اس جیسی خیانت سے سلامتی کو واجب کرتا ہے پس جب خیانت ظاہر ہوئی تو مشتری کو اختیار دیا جائے گا جیسا کہ عیب میں ہے۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی نے ایک غلام ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض ادھار خریدا پھر اس نے ایک سورو پسیہ کا نفع لے کر گیارہ سو روپیہ کے عوض مرابحہ کے طور پر نقد فروخت کر دیا اور یہ بیان نہیں کیا کہ میں نے ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض ادھار خریدا تھا پھر مشتری ثانی کو معلوم ہوا کہ مشتری اول یعنی میرے بائع نے ادھار خریدا ہے تو مشتری ثانی کو اختیار ہوگا کہ وہ بیع مرابحہ کو رد کر دے یا اس کو گیارہ سو یہ نقد کے عوض قبول کر لے۔ اس کے قائل امام شافعی اور امام احمد ہیں۔
A slave bought on credit for one thousand and sold with profit of one hundred without disclosing - then the buyer can rescind or not? He said: Whoever buys a slave for one thousand dirhams on credit, then sells it with profit of one hundred without disclosing, and the buyer learns, then if he wishes he may return it, and if he wishes he may accept, because the term has resemblance to the thing sold. Do you not see that the price is increased due to the term? And the resemblance in this is attached to the reality, so it becomes as if he bought two things and sold one of them as Murabaha at the price of both. And proceeding with Murabaha requires safety from such betrayal. So when it appears, he is given choice as in defect. Translation: If someone bought a slave on credit for one thousand dirhams, then sold it with profit of one hundred dirhams without disclosing the credit purchase, then the buyer comes to know, so if he wishes he may return it and if he wishes he may accept. Because the term resembles the sale, do you not see that price is increased due to the term? And the resemblance in this matter is attached to reality, so it becomes as if he bought two things and sold one of them as Murabaha at the price of both. And proceeding with Murabaha obligates safety from such betrayal. So when betrayal appears, the buyer is given choice as in defect. Explanation: The scenario is that a man bought a slave on credit for one thousand rupees, then took a profit of one hundred rupees and sold it as Murabaha for eleven hundred rupees cash, and did not disclose that he had bought it on credit. Then the second buyer learns that the first buyer (the seller) had bought on credit. So the second buyer has the option to rescind the Murabaha sale or accept it for eleven hundred cash. Those who hold this are Imam Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof regarding the effect of undisclosed credit in a Murabaha sale. The Hanafi school holds that credit (ajal) is similar to the thing sold because it directly affects the price; a higher price is charged when credit is given. Therefore, if a seller buys an item on credit and then sells it as Murabaha (cost-plus) without revealing that he bought it on credit, he is effectively combining two distinct considerations: the item itself and the credit. By selling the item only at the cost-plus basis without accounting for the credit, he commits betrayal (khayanat). Consequently, the second buyer has the option to either void the sale or accept it at the stated price. This option is analogous to the option due to defect (khiyar al-ayb). The text also notes that Imam Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad concur with this ruling.

Key Terms
نسيئة (nasī'ah): Sale on credit with deferred payment.
خيانة (khiyānah): Breach of trust or betrayal in a fiduciary sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person buys a slave for 1000 dirhams on credit, then sells it as Murabaha for 1100 dirhams cash without disclosing the credit. The buyer learns the truth. According to Hanafi fiqh, the buyer:
A) Must accept the sale because the item is sound.
B) Has the option to rescind or accept at 1100.
C) Can only claim the difference in price.
D) Cannot object because cash price is final.

Key Takeaway
Credit in Murabaha is treated like a commodity; non-disclosure is betrayal giving the buyer the option to rescind or accept.

Destruction of Item After Undisclosed Credit Sale
Ruling on liability after destruction of the Murabaha item.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a Murabaha buyer destroys the item before learning of undisclosed credit, and explain why the option to rescind is lost.

Core Idea
Destruction of the item terminates the right of rescission: full price is due, no part of credit is deductible.

This passage from the Hidayah addresses a specific sub-ruling in Murabaha: what happens when the second buyer destroys the item before discovering that the first buyer had purchased it on credit. The ruling clarifies that the option to rescind (khiyar) for betrayal is contingent upon the item's existence. Once the item is destroyed, the full price must be paid, because the credit period (ajal) is not an actual component that can be returned or deducted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize the Murabaha context: first buyer buys on credit, sells for cash without disclosure; second buyer destroys (sells, frees, or kills) the item before learning of the credit.
The destruction is inclusive of any act that transfers ownership or eliminates the item.
2
State the ruling
The second buyer must pay the full agreed price (1100 dirhams in the example) — he cannot rescind the sale.
3
Locate the proof (dalil)
The Hidayah states: 'because the term (ajal) is not offset by anything of the price.' This means credit is not a tangible part of the consideration; it does not diminish the price.
The proof is a direct rational statement (talil), not from Quran or hadith.
4
Understand the condition for rescission
The option to rescind due to suspicion of betrayal only applies when the item is still in existence. Once destroyed, the right is lost.
If the item were still present, the buyer could choose to rescind or pay the full price.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان استهلكه ثم علم لزمه بالف ومائة لان الاجل لايقابل شيء من الثمن ترجمہ اور اگر مشتری ثانی نے بیع کو تلف کیا پھر اس کو معلوم ہوا تو مشتری ثانی پر گیارہ سو روپیہ لازم ہونگے کیونکہ میعاد کے مقابلہ میں شمن کا کوئی حصہ نہیں ہے۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے ایک غلام ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض ادھار خریدا پھر اس کو گیارہ سورو پیر کے عوض نقد بیع مرابحہ کے طور پر بیچا اور مشتری اول نے فروخت کرتے وقت یہ بیان نہیں کیا کہ میں نے ادھار خریدا ہے پھر اس غلام کو مشتری ثانی نے ہلاک کر دیا۔ یعنی فروخت کر دیا یا آزاد کر دیا یا مار ڈالا۔ اس کے بعد مشتری ثانی کو معلوم ہوا کہ مشتری اول نے یہ غلام ادھار خریدا تھا اور مجھ کو نفقد فروخت کیا ہے تو مشتری ثانی پر گیارہ سو روپیہی لازم ہونگے کیونکہ حقیقتاً میعاد کے مقابلہ میں شمن نہیں ہوتا البتہ مقام کا شبہ ضرور ہے پس شبۂ خیانت کی وجہ سے اس کو فتح کر دینے کا اختیار دیا گیا ہے لیکن فسخ کرنا اسی وقت ممکن ہو گا جبکہ بیج موجود ہو اور جب بیج موجود نہ ہو تو میعاد کے مقابلہ میں شمن کا کوئی حصہ ساقط نہیں کیا جائے گا۔
And if he destroys it then learns, he is liable for one thousand and one hundred, because the term is not compensated by anything of the price. Translation: And if the second buyer destroys the sold item then learns, then one thousand one hundred dirhams are obligatory upon the second buyer, because the term is not offset by any part of the price. Explanation: The scenario is that someone bought a slave on credit for one thousand rupees, then sold it for eleven hundred rupees cash as Murabaha, and the first seller did not disclose at the time of sale that he had bought it on credit. Then the second buyer destroys the slave, meaning sells it, frees it, or kills it. After that, the second buyer learns that the first seller had bought the slave on credit and sold it to him for cash. Then one thousand one hundred rupees are obligatory upon the second buyer, because in reality the term is not compensated by any price. However, there is the doubt of betrayal, so due to the doubt of betrayal, he was given the option to rescind. But rescission is only possible when the item is present. When the item is not present, no part of the price is deducted against the term.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents a sub-ruling within the context of undisclosed credit in Murabaha. If the second buyer destroys the item (e.g., sells, frees, or kills) before learning of the undisclosed credit, then he cannot rescind the sale because the item no longer exists. Instead, he must pay the full agreed price (1100 dirhams). The reason is that while credit (ajal) is similar to a thing in terms of affecting the price, in reality it is not an actual item that can be returned. The suspicion of betrayal only gives the option of rescission when the item is still in existence; once destroyed, the full price is due. This ruling follows from the principle that the option of rescission (khiyar) applies only when the subject matter is extant.

Key Terms
استهلاك (istihlāk): Destruction or consumption of the item, including sale, freeing, or killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He can return a substitute.
B) He owes the full price of 1100.
C) He owes only the cost price of 1000.
D) He can rescind and pay nothing.

Key Takeaway
Full price is due after destruction; the option to rescind only exists when the item is still in existence.

Tawliyah Sale Without Disclosure and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Destruction
Intra-Hanafi dispute on destruction remedy in Tawliyah non-disclosure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Tawliyah sale without disclosure and evaluate the intra-Hanafi difference between the majority and Abu Yusuf on the destruction scenario.

Core Idea
Tawliyah disclosure is wajib; non-disclosure gives option to rescind, but on destruction, liability splits between full price (majority) and value recovery (Abu Yusuf).

This lesson delves into a specific intra-Hanafi dispute within the rules of Tawliyah. While the school agrees that non-disclosure of a credit element gives the buyer a right to rescind the sale (like Murabaha), a sharp difference emerges when the sold item is destroyed before discovery. The majority ruling and the minority view of Imam Abu Yusuf both rest on careful analogical reasoning, and understanding their arguments sharpens your legal analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and the core ruling
Tawliyah is a sale at cost. The seller must disclose if he bought the item on credit, just as in Murabaha. Non-disclosure is breach of trust and gives the buyer an option to rescind.
2
Separate the existence vs. destruction scenario
The text explicitly distinguishes: if the item still exists, the buyer may return it (rescission). If destroyed, the question becomes financial liability.
3
Study the majority Hanafi view on destruction
The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) holds that the buyer must pay the full cash price agreed. Credit is not compensable by any part of the price, so the buyer cannot reduce the payment.
4
Analyze Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Imam Abu Yusuf argues by analogy (qiyas) to counterfeit coins: just as the recipient of counterfeit coins returns their value and reclaims his payment, the buyer here returns the item's value and recovers the full price.
The text notes this as a valid analogy within the school.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical difference: if the item is destroyed, under the majority the buyer pays the full price; under Abu Yusuf, he pays only the value and gets a refund of the difference. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
The tarjih authority is not explicit in this passage, but the majority position is presented first and is relied upon in later Hanafi works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر مشتری اول نے مشتری ثانی کو تولیہ پر بیعا اور بیان نہ کیا ہو واپس کرنے کا حکم قال فان كان ولاه اياه ولم يبين رده ان شاء لان الخيانة في التولية مثلها في المرابحة لأنه بناء على الثمن الاول وان كان استهلكه ثم علم لزمه بالف حالة لما ذكرناه وعن ابى يوسف انه يرد القيمة ويسترد كل الثمن وهو نظير ما اذا استوفى الزيوف مكان الجياد و علم بعد الاتفاق و سیاتیک من بعد ان شاء الله تعالى وقيل يقوم بثمن حال وبثمن مؤجل فير جع بفضل ما بينهما ولو لم يكن الاجل مشروطافي العقد ولكنه منجم معتاد قيل لا بد من بيانه لان المعروف كالمشروط وقيل يبيعه ولا يبينه لان الثمن حال
If the first seller sold to the second buyer by Tawliyah and did not disclose, the ruling on return. He said: If he transferred (wala) it to him and did not disclose, he may return it if he wishes, because the betrayal in Tawliyah is like that in Murabaha, since it is based on the first price. And if he destroyed it then learned, he is liable for the full price in cash, due to what we mentioned. And from Abu Yusuf: he returns the value and takes back the entire price. This is analogous to the case when one receives counterfeit coins instead of good coins and learns after spending. And it will come later, if Allah wills. And it is said: He appraises it at a cash price and at a deferred price, then he returns the difference between them. And even if the term was not stipulated in the contract but is customarily deferred, it is said: It must be disclosed because the customary is like stipulated. And it is said: He sells it without disclosing because the price is cash.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding Tawliyah (sale at cost) without disclosure. According to the majority Hanafi position, the buyer has the option to rescind, similar to Murabaha, because betrayal (khayanat) exists when the seller conceals the fact that he bought the item on credit. If the item is destroyed, the buyer pays the full cash price. However, Imam Abu Yusuf holds a different view: the buyer should return the item's value and recover the entire price he paid. This is analogous to the case where someone receives counterfeit coins and later discovers it. Another opinion suggests that the item should be appraised at both cash and credit prices, and the buyer returns the difference. The text also discusses whether customary deferment requires disclosure; one view says yes (custom is like stipulation), another says no because the price is cash. This reflects the nuanced Hanafi methodology of balancing textual ruling with custom.

Key Terms
تولیہ (tawliyah): A sale at cost, without profit, where the seller transfers the item at the price he paid.
قیمت (qīmah): Market value or replacement value of the item.
خیانة (khayānah): Betrayal or breach of trust, here referring to non-disclosure of credit in a trust-based sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, if the buyer in a Tawliyah sale destroys the item and then learns of undisclosed credit, he must:
A) Pay the full cash price.
B) Return the item's value and get his money back.
C) Appraise and pay the difference.
D) Rescind the sale without payment.

Key Takeaway
In Tawliyah, non-disclosure of credit gives the buyer an option to rescind; if the item is destroyed, the majority Hanafi position holds the buyer liable for the full price.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Murabahah with Undisclosed Credit and Destruction of Goods
Ruling on murabahah with concealed credit and subsequent destruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that ajal does not reduce the price, and determine the buyer's liability when goods are destroyed in a murabahah sale with concealed credit.

Core Idea
Credit term does not reduce price; full cash price is due even after destruction.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in murabahah (cost-plus sale): what happens when the seller conceals that he bought the item on credit, and the buyer destroys the item before discovering the concealment. The Hanafi school establishes that the buyer remains liable for the full cash price because the credit period does not correspond to any part of the price. Understanding this principle helps avoid confusion between price and payment terms in deferred sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and the concealment
The seller purchased a slave on credit for 1000, then sold it in a murabahah for 1100 cash without disclosing the credit. Note the two prices: cost price (1000 credit) and sale price (1100 cash).
Concealment of credit is considered khiyanah (fraud).
2
Determine the buyer's initial option
Upon learning of the concealment while the goods exist, the buyer has the option to rescind the sale or accept it for the full cash price. This is analogous to the option for a defect.
The option exists only while goods are present.
3
Apply the scenario of destruction
If the buyer destroys the goods (by selling, freeing, or killing) before learning the truth, the option is lost. The buyer must pay the full 1100.
Destruction here means any act that removes the goods from existence.
4
State the legal basis
The proof: 'because the term does not correspond to any part of the price' (lā yuqābiluhu shay' min al-thaman). In Hanafi fiqh, ajal is a separate condition, not a portion of the price.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the nature of credit.
5
Conclude the liability
Therefore, the second buyer must pay 1100. No reduction is allowed because the term was never part of the price.
The full cash price is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text

Page 272. Book of Sales. The commentary Ashraf al-Hidayah in Urdu attaches to Hidayah volume eight. So here, the term (credit) which is attached to the sale will be attached to the actual sale. Now it became as if the first buyer purchased two things for one thousand rupees: a slave and another thing, the term. And among them, he sold the slave with a profit of one hundred rupees, in exchange for the price of both, i.e., one thousand rupees is the price of both the slave and the term, but he sold only the slave for one thousand rupees and took an extra profit of one hundred rupees, and this is forbidden and fraudulent. And it has passed earlier that in a murabahah sale, it is necessary to avoid both fraud and the appearance of fraud. So in this issue, proceeding with a murabahah sale necessitates avoiding such fraud. But when the first seller did not disclose, and later the fraud appeared, the second buyer has the option to return the sale or accept it for one thousand one hundred rupees cash, just as after the appearance of a defect in a sale, the buyer has the option. And if the first buyer bought a slave on credit for one thousand rupees, then sold it for one thousand one hundred in murabahah, and the second buyer destroyed the slave, then the credit was known, so one thousand one hundred are due on the second buyer. The text: 'And if he destroyed it, then he knows, one thousand and one hundred are due on him, because the term does not correspond to any part of the price.' Translation: And if the second buyer destroyed the sold item, then he knows, one thousand one hundred rupees will be due on the second buyer, because the term does not correspond to any part of the price. Explanation: The issue is that if someone bought a slave on credit for one thousand rupees, then sold it for one thousand one hundred cash as a murabahah sale, and the first seller did not mention at the time of sale that he had bought it on credit, then the second buyer destroyed the slave—meaning he sold it, freed it, or killed it. After that, the second buyer learns that the first buyer had bought this slave on credit and sold it to him for cash. Then one thousand one hundred rupees will be due on the second buyer because in reality, the term does not correspond to any price; however, there is definitely a doubt about the appearance [of fraud]. So due to the appearance of fraud, he is given the option to annul, but annulment is only possible when the goods exist. And when the goods do not exist, no part of the price is dropped because of the term.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage states a ruling from the chapter of sales regarding murabahah (cost-plus sale) when the seller conceals that he purchased the item on credit. The passage begins by explaining that when a seller buys an item on credit (e.g., for 1000 rupees) and then sells it in a murabahah sale at a profit (e.g., for 1100 rupees) without disclosing the credit, the second buyer has the option: he may either annul the sale or accept it for the full 1100 rupees cash. This is analogous to the option (khiyar) given when a defect is discovered. The text then introduces the specific case: if the second buyer destroys the item (by selling, freeing, or killing) before learning about the credit, and then discovers the concealment, he is still liable for the full 1100 rupees. The proof provided is: 'because the term (ajal) does not correspond to any part of the price.' The commentary elaborates that in reality, the extension of time does not reduce the price; the price is solely for the goods. The appearance of fraud (shubhah khiyanah) gives the second buyer the right to rescind the contract only while the goods exist. Once the goods are destroyed, that option ceases, and the full cash price becomes binding. This ruling is based on the Hanafi principle that in sales involving credit, the credit period itself is not a compensable part of the price; it merely postpones payment. Therefore, the second buyer must pay the stated cash amount as if the credit were never a factor. The passage guards against the misunderstanding that credit could reduce the price or that destruction of goods would relieve the buyer from paying the full amount.

Key Terms
بيع مرابحة (bay' murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a specified profit.
اجل (ajal): The deferred payment period (credit term).
خيانة (khiyanah): Fraud, particularly concealing a defect (here, the credit) that affects the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a murabahah sale, if the seller bought goods on credit for 1000 and sold for 1100 cash without disclosing the credit, and the buyer destroyed the goods before learning the truth, how much is the buyer liable for?
A) 1000
B) 1100
C) 1050
D) Nothing, because the sale is void

Key Takeaway
The credit term does not reduce the price; therefore, in a murabahah with concealed credit, the buyer must pay the full cash price even after destroying the goods.

Tawliyah Sale: Undisclosed Credit and Destruction of Goods
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tawliyah with destroyed goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the primary Hanafi ruling when goods are destroyed after undisclosed credit in tawliyah, and distinguish between the views of Abu Yusuf and Abu Ja'far.

Core Idea
When tawliyah goods are destroyed after concealed credit, the sale binds at cash price; but Abu Yusuf and Abu Ja'far propose alternative compensations.

This passage examines a scenario where a seller buys goods on credit, then sells them as tawliyah (at cost) without disclosing the credit. If the buyer discovers the deceit after the goods are destroyed, what must he pay? The primary Hanafi ruling makes the sale binding at the cash price, yet several Hanafi masters offer different remedies. We will walk through the main ruling and the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the initial option
First, recognize that if the goods still exist, the buyer has the right to return them and recover his payment because non-disclosure creates fraud (khiyanah).
This option is common to both murabahah and tawliyah.
2
Understand the primary ruling for destroyed goods
If the goods are destroyed before the buyer learns of the credit, the sale becomes binding at the full cash price (1000). The reason: the term does not reduce the price, and annulment is impossible after destruction.
The evidence is that the term corresponds to no part of the price.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's alternative view
Abu Yusuf holds that the buyer returns the value (qimah) of the destroyed goods (which may differ from the price) and recovers his full payment. This is analogized to returning counterfeit coins.
Value stands in place of the goods.
4
Analyze Abu Ja'far's alternative view
Abu Ja'far says the goods are assessed at both cash price and deferred price; the buyer returns the difference between the two valuations.
This view includes a fictional appraisal at two prices.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the primary view the buyer pays a fixed cash price; under Abu Yusuf the payment varies with market value; under Abu Ja'far the buyer only pays the difference between the two appraisals.
The dispute affects the buyer's financial risk.

Source Text
